    Discovery of BAY-298 and BAY-899: Tetrahydro-1,6-naphthyridine-Based, Potent, and Selective Antagonists of the Luteinizing Hormone Receptor Which Reduce Sex Hormone Levels in Vivo

    Abstract

    The human luteinizing hormone receptor (hLH-R) is a member of the glycoprotein hormone family of G-protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs), activated by luteinizing hormone (hLH) and essentially involved in the regulation of sex hormone production. Thus, hLH-R represents a valid target for the treatment of sex hormone-dependent cancers and diseases (polycystic ovary syndrome, uterine fibroids, endometriosis) as well as contraception. Screening of the Bayer compound library led to the discovery of tetrahydrothienopyridine derivatives as novel, small-molecule (SMOL) hLH-R inhibitors and to the development of BAY-298, the first nanomolar hLH-R antagonist reducing sex hormone levels in vivo. Further optimization of physicochemical, pharmacokinetic, and safety parameters led to the identification of BAY-899 with an improved in vitro profile and proven efficacy in vivo. BAY-298 and BAY-899 serve as valuable tool compounds to study hLH-R signaling in vitro and to interfere with the production of sex hormones in vivo.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The hypothalamic–pituitary–gonadal (HPG) axis is the key pathway for the production of the sex hormones estradiol and progesterone in females, as well as testosterone in males.(1) Sex hormone synthesis is initiated via binding of gonadotropin-releasing hormone (GnRH) to its cognate receptor (GnRH-R) leading to receptor activation and the expression and secretion of two gonadotropins, namely, follicle-stimulating hormone (FSH) and luteinizing hormone (LH). Binding of these glycoprotein hormones to their specific downstream receptors, the FSH and LH receptors (FSH-R, LH-R), then stimulates the production of sex hormones in both ovaries and testes. The actual sex hormone levels produced are tightly controlled by several negative feedback loops.(2,3) Given the essential role of sex hormones in a wide variety of developmental and physiological processes, an impressive number of strategies aimed at the modulation of their actual levels and function have been developed to treat hormone-dependent diseases such as certain types of cancers,(4) uterine fibroids,(5) polycystic ovary syndrome (PCOS),(6) and endometriosis,(7) as well as for female(8) and male contraception.(9) Thus, interfering with hLH-R-dependent hormone production with small-molecule (SMOL) antagonists represents a valid approach not only to study sex hormone physiology but also to tackle sex hormone-dependent disease conditions or contraception.
    Based on several characteristic features, the hLH-R has been assigned to the δ-group of the rhodopsin family of G-protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs)(10) and belongs to the glycoprotein hormone receptor cluster, together with the hFSH-R and thyroid-stimulating hormone receptor (TSH-R). Whereas most rhodopsin receptor ligands bind to a rhodopsin-like binding cavity within the 7-transmembrane region (7TMR),(11) the glycoprotein hormone receptors are activated by high-affinity agonists binding to a large, glycosylated extracellular domain. Besides FSH, gonadal sex hormone production is predominantly regulated by pituitary-derived LH, a noncovalently linked, heterodimeric glycoprotein consisting of an α- and a β-subunit, the α-subunit being common to all glycoprotein hormones. Whereas agonist–receptor binding first and foremost induces cAMP second messenger signaling,(12) it also results in Ca2+ release as a secondary coupling mechanism.(13) Receptor activation has also been linked to additional downstream signaling processes(14) and high organ responsiveness,(15) adding further to complexity.
    Two tool compound classes for the LH receptor have been described. Heitman et al.(16) reported in vitro studies with the LH receptor agonist Org 43553(17) and the LH receptor antagonist LUF5771(18) (Figure 1).
    These workers(16) convincingly demonstrated that Org 43553 reversibly binds to an allosteric binding site in the hLH-R without interfering with the binding of the natural ligand LH. Furthermore, follow-up studies(19) by the same group indicated the existence of a second, distinct allosteric binding site (i.e., different from the Org 43553 binding site) to which the antagonist LUF5771 binds, blocking the Org 43553 agonist activity. However, no SMOL hLH-R antagonist suitable for in vivo application has been described so far. Thus, we decided to initiate a lead finding program for this target and now report the identification of the tetrahydro-1,6-naphthyridine-based compounds BAY-298 and BAY-899 as potent and selective antagonists of the hLH-R with a good pharmacokinetic profile and proven activity in several in vivo models.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    High-Throughput Screening and Identification of Screening Hit 1

    High-throughput screening (HTS) was used to identify SMOL antagonists of the hLH receptor. A total of 1.19 million compounds were screened using frozen cells(20) expressing the hLH-R, quantifying the changes in agonist-induced cAMP levels using the time-resolved fluorescence resonance energy transfer (TR-FRET) technology(21−23) (see the Supporting Information for full details). After removal of compounds interfering with the readout format, the remaining hits were checked for the absence of any unspecific cAMP modulation via interference with adenylyl cyclase activity using forskolin-stimulated HEK293 membranes (see the Supporting Information for assay details). Following a thorough hit-to-lead examination adressing additional off-target, selectivity and species specificity properties, and the removal of compounds with pan-assay interference motifs,(24) tetrahydrothienopyridine-based compound 1 (Table 1) was identified as lead structure.
    Table 1. Analysis of the Role of the Absolute Configuration of 1a
      IC50 [nM]
    compoundabsolute configurationhLHrLHmLHhFSHhTSH

    a

    IC50 values are arithmetic means of at least two independent experiments run in duplicates.

    Early profiling of 1 further revealed no residual hLH-R agonist activity as well as no antagonist activity in a panel of closely related glycoprotein hormone and leucine-rich-repeat-containing GPCRs (hFSH-R, hTSH-R, hRXFP1/hLGR7-R). In addition, 1 was active at the rat LH receptor (rLH-R) with a similar potency, yet inactive at the corresponding murine LH receptor (mLH-R; see Table 1). Compound 1 was of moderate potency (IC50 (hLH) = 378 nM) and exhibited borderline Lipinski rule of five properties,(25) being suboptimal for an oral route of administration. In particular, the lipophilicity of 1 (log D = 5, TPSA = 42) was considered to be high. At the same time, the Caco-2 permeability was low (<1 nm/s for Papp(A–B)). Therefore, we carefully monitored log D during compound optimization, even though reduction of lipophilicity while simultaneously improving potency was considered challenging as we assumed binding of our ligand to the lipophilic 7TMR.

    Synthesis of Screening Hit 1

    Compound 1 was synthesized as outlined in Scheme 1. Following a literature procedure,(26) key intermediate 4 was prepared in racemic form via a Pictet–Spengler reaction from commercially available 2-(thiophen-2-yl)ethan-1-amine (2) and 4-methoxybenzaldehyde (3). Subsequent reaction of 4 with 1,3-dichloro-5-isocyanatobenzene furnished urea 1. This short and efficient route allowed the introduction of a broad scope of variations by using different benzaldehydes and different capping reactions to functionalize the secondary amine in compound 4.

    Structure–Activity Relationship and Profiling of the hLH-R Antagonists BAY-298 and BAY-899

    We first investigated the role of the absolute configuration of 1 (Table 1). Thus, racemate 1 was separated into its enantiomers 1a and 1b, with subsequent tests demonstrating that 1a is the physiologically relevant enantiomer (hLH-R ∼17 times, rLH-R ∼12 times more active than 1b). We were able to obtain diffracting crystals of a closely related derivative of eutomer 1a, namely, compound 5 (Figure 2) with the (S)-configuration determined by X-ray analysis.
    Next, we investigated the structure–activity relationship of the substituent at the 4-position of tetrahydrothienopyridines (see Table 2). Removal of the p-methoxy group of 1, which could be a potential metabolic weak spot, led to significantly reduced activity (see compound 6). Investigation of other para substituents (compounds 11–14, 19), however, clearly demonstrated that target activity was retained upon replacement of the methoxy by a chloro substituent (compound 14). All attempts to lower log D values by the introduction of a polar group were detrimental to activity (compare compounds 7–9, 18, 19). Investigation of selected compounds in a human liver microsome assay revealed low to moderate metabolic stability (for classification of the results, the following criteria were used: Fmax > 70% (=CL < 0.4 L/(h kg)) was classified as high metabolic stability, Fmax 30–70% (CL 0.4–0.9 L/(h kg)) as moderate stability, and Fmax < 30% (CL > 0.9 L/(h kg)) as low metabolic stability). A halogen substituent at the para position of the 4-phenyl group seemed to increase metabolic stability significantly (see compounds 13–15).
    Table 2. SAR Investigation of the Substituent at the 4-Positiona
    a

    IC50 values are arithmetic means of at least two independent experiments run in duplicates.

    The results of our SAR investigation of the urea capping group at the 5-position are depicted in Table 3. Both chloro atoms in 1 could be replaced either by methyl (compound 20) or by methoxy (compound 21), reducing log D values while maintaining target activity. Removal of one (compare compounds 1 and 22) or both (compare compounds 14 and 23) chloro atoms led to a significantly lower log D value while retaining LH antagonism. A sterically constrained para substituent (compound 24) or a combination of two lipophilic meta and para substituents (compounds 25, 26) was tolerated without loss of target activity. In the case of polar substituents, the target potency was dramatically reduced (compounds 27, 28). To our surprise, even a sterically bulky lipophilic substituent at the para or meta position was tolerated (compounds 29, 30) but led to a significant increase in the log D value. With regard to metabolic stability in human liver microsomes, a combination of R1 = Cl and a para-substituted R2 group led to increased stability of 26 and 30 (Table 3).
    Table 3. SAR Investigation of the Substituent at the 5-Positiona
    a

    IC50 values are arithmetic means of at least two independent experiments run in duplicates.

    The SAR data compiled in Table 4 underscored the importance of the urea moiety, indicating that both the carbonyl and the NH groups are essential for target activity. Thus, 31 with a thiourea moiety is about 16 times less active than 21, implying that the carbonyl group might be incorporated in a binding interaction with the hLH-R. Replacement of the urea NH group by CH2 (compound 32) led to a complete loss of activity (compare compound 20, Table 3).
    Table 4. SAR Investigation of Urea Capping Moiety Aa
    a

    IC50 values are arithmetic means of at least two independent experiments run in duplicates.

    As thiophenes are known to carry a high risk for formation of reactive metabolites(27) and to further decrease metabolic stability in human liver microsomes, we investigated potential replacement options for the thiophene moiety (see “A” ring, Table 5). In doing so, we observed that replacement of the thiophene in 30 (see Table 3) by a pyridine (see compound 33, Table 5) resulted in retained target activity while significantly improving the metabolic stability in human liver microsomes.
    Table 5. SAR Investigation of Hetaryl Ring “A” and Identification of 33 (BAY-298) and Chemical Probe 36 (BAY-899)a
    compoundRYZIC50 (hLH) [nM]exp. log Dcalc. log DCL hLM [L/(h kg)]Fmax hLM [%]
    33 (BAY-298)ClCHCH964.74.50.285
    34FCHCH1023.94.20.375
    35FCHN3123.23.50.283
    36 (BAY-899)FNN1852.83.00.189
    a

    IC50 values are arithmetic means of at least two independent experiments run in duplicates.

    Given these results, we decided to further profile 33 (BAY-298) as a potential chemical probe(28) for the hLH-R both in vitro and in vivo (with the results obtained summarized in Figures 3 and 4). Compound 33 showed potent antagonism at the human, rat, and cynomolgus monkey LH-R (see Figure 3) and was expected to be inactive at the respective mouse receptor, as previously shown for 1. Likewise, 33 did not show any residual hLH agonism, was inactive in an hFSH-R assay, and exhibited only marginal (i.e., ∼24-fold selective) hTSH-R antagonist activity.
    Compound 33 is characterized by good in vitro pharmacological, physicochemical, safety, and DMPK property profiles (Figure 3). With regard to safety, 33 was moderately active in the hERG assay (IC50 = 3 μM) and had a mixed CYP inhibition profile (no effect on isoforms 1A2, 2D6, and 3A4 but significant inhibition of 2C8 and 2C9). Compound 33 performed particularly well in both human and rat liver microsomes and also rat hepatocyte assays, indicative of a low clearance. In addition, it showed good permeability and no efflux in a Caco-2 assay system. In vivo, these properties translated to a low-dose pharmacokinetic profile in rats with low clearance, long half-life, and a high volume of distribution resulting in a good oral bioavailability and high exposure (see Figure 4).
    Our continued efforts to further improve the properties of 33 (BAY-298) by lowering the log D value led to the discovery of 36 (BAY-899; see Table 5). The log D value could be lowered by almost two log units; in turn, compound 36 was about 10 times more soluble than 33, had better permeability in the Caco-2 assay, and showed an improved CYP inhibition as well as hERG profile (compare Figures 3 and 5). In addition, the selectivity versus the TSH receptor could be further increased to >100-fold. Investigation of 36 in a low-dose pharmacokinetic study in rats also revealed a low clearance, a long half-life, and a high volume of distribution, resulting in a good oral bioavailability and high exposure (see Figure 4). Both compounds (33 and 36) were investigated in a cell-based panel of 25 GPCRs (see SI), none of which activated or inhibited a GPCR >70% at 10 μM compound concentration, indicating an excellent selectivity within the target family. 33 and 36 both exhibited an appealing in vitro and in vivo profile with 36 bearing superior properties in several assays tested as discussed above. Thus, 36 (BAY-899) meets the criteria for chemical probes(28) established by the Structural Genomics Consortium (SGC)(29) and was handed over to the SGC as a “donated chemical probe”. The less active enantiomer (data not shown) might serve as a negative control compound for future pharmacological experiments.

    Synthesis of 33 (BAY-298) and Chemical Probe 36 (BAY-899)

    The synthesis of 33 (BAY-298) is outlined in Scheme 2. Piperidone intermediate 40 was synthesized in two steps from commercially available 4-chlorophenylmagnesium bromide (37), benzyl chloroformate (38), and 4-methoxypyridine (39), following a method described by Di Fabio et al.,(30) with subsequent zinc reduction(31) of the resulting double bond. A gold-catalyzed reaction(32) with propargylamine furnished naphthyridine 41 as a mixture with the undesired regioisomer 42. The mixture of compounds 41 and 42 could be converted into 33 via deprotection of the carboxybenzyl protecting group, subsequent urea formation using 4-nitrophenyl chloroformate as a reagent, and separation of the regioisomers and enantiomers. The synthesis of chemical probe 36 (BAY-899) followed a similar route.

    Mode of Action Studies

    Antagonist Dose–Response Studies in the Presence of Multiples of the EC80 Concentration of either hLH or the SMOL hLH-R Agonist Org 43553

    To shed light on the mode of action, we performed antagonist dose–response studies with 33 (BAY-298) in the presence of four different, single concentrations of the natural ligand hLH (Figure 6A) or the SMOL hLH-R agonist Org 43553 (Figure 6B) using the results obtained at 1 × EC80 as control.
    In both cases (Figure 6A,B), the antagonist efficacy of 33 remained largely unaffected and potency values clearly showed a limited rightward shift in dose–response indicative of a saturation in antagonist activity not being affected by agonist. Thus, we conclude that 33 binds to a site different from the actual agonist binding sites of hLH and Org 43553. In both cases, 33 behaved like a negative allosteric modulator of agonist-induced hLH-R activity. The finding that the antagonist activity of 33 is neither affected by the binding of hLH nor by the SMOL hLH-R agonist Org 43553 is well in line with previous results. Heitman et al.(18,19) not only demonstrated reversible binding of Org 43553 to a putative rhodopsin-like SMOL binding cavity(11) but also validated the existence of a second, independent SMOL binding site for both positive and negative modulators of Org 43553 hLH-R agonist activity in close vicinity. Future studies will be necessary to finally clarify whether 33 binds in a similar way(18,33) or to an unrelated, nonoverlapping site within the “ligand-binding landscape” of the δ-subgroup of rhodopsin receptors.(34)

    Short- and Long-Term Effects of the Treatment of Female and Male Rats with 33 (BAY-298) and 36 (BAY-899)

    On the basis of the promising pharmacokinetic results obtained, we further analyzed the effects of our SMOL LH-R antagonists in vivo. LH receptor signaling rapidly induces specific gene expression cascades, initiates ovulation in the female, and regulates testosterone synthesis in the male. Therefore, the effects of 33 (BAY-298) both on testosterone synthesis in male rats and ovulatory marker genes and on progesterone plasma concentrations in female rats were evaluated. LH-induced gene expression was studied in female rats following treatment with cetrorelix (a competitive GnRH-R antagonist), which completely blocks the animal’s HPG axis and thus endogenous FSH and LH synthesis and secretion. Intravenous administration of hLH (0.5 μg/animal, 2.27 μg/kg) led to a significant increase in the mRNA levels of known LH-dependent marker genes (i.e., COX-2, Egr-1, and RUNX-1),(35) whereas treatment with 33, applied simultaneously iv, led to a dose-dependent decrease in their respective mRNA levels (Figure 7).
    In accordance with published data,(36) administration of hLH also reduced the expression of aromatase (CYP19A1) mRNA in the same animal model. This effect was counteracted by treatment with 33 in a dose-dependent manner (Figure 8A). In addition, a marked reduction in plasma progesterone concentrations in comparison to control animals treated with hLH only was demonstrated (Figure 8B).
    Likewise, iv administration of hLH (0.1 μg and 0.3 μg/animal, i.e., 0.45 and 1.36 μg/kg, respectively) led to a rapid, dose-dependent increase in plasma testosterone levels in cetrorelix-treated male rats 2 h after treatment (Figure 9).(37) These levels were significantly lowered in the presence of 33 (BAY-298), simultaneously applied iv at 3 mg/animal (i.e., 14 mg/kg).
    Taken together, the results demonstrate that treatment with 33 rapidly blocks short-term effects of LH signaling with respect to the expression of known LH receptor-induced genes and by lowering plasma hormone levels in male and female rats in a dose-dependent manner.
    Therefore, we proceeded to an investigation of long-term effects in naturally cycling female rats upon treatment with 33. Checks for changes in the estrus cycle as well as effects on estradiol levels were made. In contrast to a fertile cycle of 28 days in women, the estrus cycle of female rats lasts only 4 days and can be split into four stages, all of which can be routinely monitored via the examination of vaginal smears(38) (i.e., rapid follicle development (diestrus), ovulation (proestrus), formation of corpora lutea and progesterone production (estrus), and the regression of unfertilized corpora lutea (metestrus)). Cycle-staged rats were treated orally with 33 for 8 days and monitored for two cycles (Figure 10). Whereas control animals cycled normally, animals receiving 33 once daily showed cycle arrest in diestrus and metestrus in a dose-dependent manner, in analogy to the results obtained with LH-R knockout mice(39) and in line with known hLH-R mutations in women.(40)
    Serum estradiol levels were measured after the 8 day treatments and found to be dose-dependently lowered compared to vehicle-treated rats in proestrus, the day of highest estradiol concentrations (Figure 11A). Plasma concentrations on day 8 were in the range of 4–39 μM, increased according to the different dose groups (4.5–72 mg/kg). Given a fraction unbound of 0.24%, a plasma concentration of 10 μM results in an unbound plasma concentration of 24 nM, which is close to the IC50 value of 23 nM. Therefore, the obtained plasma concentrations fit to the general rules for dose selections, which can be summarized as follows: Based on in vitro potency, PK data, and protein binding, the dosing for in vivo studies should result in free plasma concentrations in the range of the in vitro IC50 values. Higher and lower doses are then selected for dose–response studies. Collectively, these experimental data demonstrate that 33 is a potent antagonist of LH-R-mediated physiological functions in vivo, both in the ovary and testis. Compound 33 is equally suited for use in long-term studies addressing the physiological effects of LH-R blockade in reproductive biology and/or pathophysiological conditions.
    In an additional study, 36 (BAY-899) was tested for its ability to reduce serum estradiol levels in intact female rats (oral application for 8 days, 12.5 mg/kg, q.d.), and showed an efficiency comparable to 33 (BAY-298) (Figure 11B). It is also worth noting that two animals of the BAY-899-treated group and three animals of the BAY-298-treated cohort again showed signs in vaginal smears of disturbed estrus cycles. However, given typically high individual variations in natural cycling rats adding to the variability between studies in this model, results should only compared within one experiment. Plasma concentrations on day 8 were 7 ± 2 μM for compound 33 (BAY-298) and 34 ± 19 μM for compound 36 (BAY-899), which is in accordance with the pharmacokinetic properties of both compounds. Thus, 36 (BAY-899) and 33 (BAY-298) can be regarded as being of comparable efficacy in in vivo studies, as expected from in vitro and pharmacokinetic data. No adverse events or undesired effects (e.g., loss of body weight or lower activity) were observed in in vivo studies with compounds 33 and 36.

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Comprehensive hit-to-lead activities led to the identification of compound 33 (BAY-298) and chemical probe 36 (BAY-899) and their characterization as the first potent and selective LH receptor antagonists suitable for in vivo administration. Both compounds are active at the human, rat, and cynomolgus monkey LH receptors in vitro. They act in an allosteric manner, do not compete with the binding of agonists to the hLH-R, and most likely bind to a putative rhodopsin-like SMOL binding cavity located within the 7-transmembrane region of the hLH-R. Both compounds have druglike properties. In comparison to BAY-298, BAY-899 does not inhibit hTSH-R signaling (>100-fold selectivity), and is significantly less lipophilic with an improved water solubility, CYP inhibition, and hERG profile. In vivo pharmacokinetics of both compounds revealed a low clearance, a high volume of distribution, and a long half-life resulting in good oral bioavailability and a high exposure.
    Compound 33 (BAY-298) demonstrated efficacy in different animal models in female and male rats. In short-term experiments, for example, 33 dose-dependently reduced LH-induced gene expression in the ovary and testosterone production after LH administration in male rats. Furthermore, once daily oral application was sufficient in long-term studies in female rats to demonstrate dose-dependent effects on plasma estradiol concentrations and the estrus cycle.
    Recent pipeline news indicates an unchanged high medical need for novel antihormonal treatment options. For example, Arvinas has moved ARV-110, the first androgen receptor-targeted proteolysis targeting chimera (PROTAC), to phase 1 clinical trials;(41) AstraZeneca is testing AZD9496, a new oral, selective estrogen receptor degrader, in advanced breast cancers;(42) and Bayer has been investigating vilaprisan, a selective progesterone receptor modulator, in phase 3 clinical trials for the treatment of gynecological diseases such as endometriosis and uterine fibroids.(43) Thus, BAY-298 (33) and BAY-899 (36) represent valuable chemical tool compounds to study LH receptor-mediated effects on sex hormones and can be used in long-term experiments to examine the receptor’s involvement in reproductive biology or pathophysiological conditions.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Chemistry

    General Methods

    All reagents and solvents were used as purchased, unless otherwise specified. All final products were at least 95% pure, as determined by ultraperformance liquid chromatography (UPLC).
    Compound names
    ICS software was used to generate IUPAC compound names. NMR spectra were recorded as solutions in DMSO-d6 or CDCl3 on Bruker Avance III HD spectrometers operating at 300 or 400 MHz for 1H and 101 MHz for 13C acquisitions. Chemical shifts (δ) are given in parts per million (ppm), and coupling constants (J) are in hertz (Hz). Spin multiplicities are reported as: s = singlet, d = doublet, t = triplet, q = quartet, m = multiplet, br = broad. HR-MS images were recorded on an MS Waters XEVO G2XS with Acquity i-Class UPLC, MS images on an MS Waters SQD 2 coupled to an Acquity UPLC, and optical rotations on a JASCO P2000 polarimeter.
    rac-N-(3,5-Dichlorophenyl)-4-(4-methoxyphenyl)-6,7-dihydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine-5(4H)-carboxamide (1)
    A solution of 4-(4-methoxyphenyl)-4,5,6,7-tetrahydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine (4; 2.00 g, 8.15 mmol; commercially available, CAS-RN: [213462-19-2]), 1,3-dichloro-5-isocyanatobenzene (1.53 g, 8.15 mmol; CAS-RN: [34893-92-0]), and triethylamine (3.4 mL) in DMSO (30 mL) was stirred at 70 °C overnight. The reaction mixture was poured into water and filtered (brown slime). The residue was dissolved in EtOAc, dried over Na2SO4, and the solvent was removed under reduced pressure. Column chromatography (silica gel, hexanes/EtOAc) gave 1. Yield 920 mg (26%). 1H NMR (300 MHz, DMSO-d6): δ = 8.96 (s, 1H, NH), 7.59 (d, J = 1.88 Hz, 2H, dichlorophenyl), 7.35 (d, J = 5.27 Hz, 1H, thiophene), 7.06–7.14 (m, 3H, methoxyphenyl, dichlorophenyl), 6.86 (d, J = 8.85 Hz, 2H, methoxyphenyl), 6.74 (d, J = 5.27 Hz, 1H, thiophene), 6.44 (s, 1H, CHN), 4.13–4.24 (m, 1H, CH2N), 3.68 (s, 3H, OMe), 2.81–3.12 (m, 3H, CH2CH2). 13C NMR (101 MHz, DMSO-d6): δ = 158.7, 153.9, 143.1, 134.5, 134.3, 133.7, 133.3, 129.2, 126.4, 123.7, 120.9, 117.5, 113.8, 55.1, 54.3, 37.8, 24.9. MS (ESI): m/z = 433.4 [M + H]+.
    (4S)-(+)-N-(3,5-Dichlorophenyl)-4-(4-methoxyphenyl)-6,7-dihydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine-5(4H)-carboxamide (1a)
    Obtained via HPLC separation of the enantiomers of 1 (column: Chiralpak IA 5 μm, 150 mm × 4.6 mm column; solvent A: hexanes, B: 2-propanol; 90% A + 10% B; flow rate: 1 mL/min): tR (min) = 8.82. [α]D20 + 261.0 ± 0.10 (c 1, DMSO).
    (4R)-(−)-N-(3,5-Dichlorophenyl)-4-(4-methoxyphenyl)-6,7-dihydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine-5(4H)-carboxamide (1b)
    Obtained via HPLC separation of the enantiomers of 1 (conditions as above): tR (min) = 7.65. [α]D20 – 301.0 ± 0.06 (c 1, DMSO).
    (4S)-(+)-N-(3,5-Dimethoxyphenyl)-4-phenyl-6,7-dihydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine-5(4H)-carboxamide (5)
    Prepared analogously to 1, from 4-phenyl-4,5,6,7-tetrahydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine (commercially available, CAS-RN: [91477-84-8]) and 1-isocyanato-3,5-dimethoxybenzene (CAS-RN: [54132-76-2]). Analytical HPLC (system: Dionex 680 pump, Dionex ASI 100, Waters 2487 UV detector; Chiralpak IA 5 μm, 150 mm × 4.6 mm column; eluent: hexanes/2-propanol 60:40 + 0.1% diethylamine, flow: 1 mL/min): tR (min) = 5.52 ((S)-(+)-enantiomer), 6.66 ((R)-(−)-enantiomer). [α]D20 + 203.0 ± 0.45 (c 1, MeOH). 1H NMR (400 MHz, CDCl3): δ = 7.29–7.40 (m, 5H, phenyl), 7.13 (d, J = 5.05 Hz, 1H, thiophene), 6.71 (d, J = 5.31 Hz, 1H, thiophene), 6.54 (d, J = 2.27 Hz, 2H, dimethoxyphenyl), 6.41 (s, 1H, dimethoxyphenyl), 6.30 (s, 1H, CHN), 6.16 (m, 1H, NH), 4.22 (m, 1H, CH2CH2), 3.76 (s, 6H, OMe), 3.37–3.46 (m, 1H, CH2CH2), 2.99–3.13 (m, 1H, CH2CH2), 2.89–2.97 (m, 1H, CH2CH2). MS (ESI): m/z = 395.0 [M + H]+.

    rac-N-(3,5-Dichlorophenyl)-4-(pyridin-2-yl)-6,7-dihydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine-5(4H)-carboxamide (7)
    Step 1: A solution of pyridine-2-carbaldehyde (1.00 g, 9.34 mmol; commercially available, CAS-RN: [1121-60-4]), 2-(thiophen-2-yl)ethan-1-amine (2; 1.1 mL, 9.3 mmol; commercially available, CAS-RN: [30433-91-1]), and triethylamine (2.06 g) in EtOH (20 mL) was stirred at rt overnight. The volatiles were removed under reduced pressure, and the resulting crude 1-(pyridin-2-yl)-N-[2-(thiophen-2-yl)ethyl]methanimine was used without further purification in the next step. Step 2: To TFA (10 mL) at rt was added 1-(pyridin-2-yl)-N-[2-(thiophen-2-yl)ethyl]methanimine (1.50 g, 6.93 mmol), and the reaction mixture was stirred at rt overnight, then concentrated under reduced pressure and poured into a mixture of saturated NaHCO3 solution and ice. After extraction with EtOAc (3×), the combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, filtered, and the solvent was removed under reduced pressure. The crude product was purified by flash column chromatography (silica gel, EtOAc/hexanes 2:8–1:1, then MeOH/DCM 1:9) to give rac-4-(pyridin-2-yl)-4,5,6,7-tetrahydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine. Yield 362 mg. Step 3: 7 was prepared analogously to 1, from 4-(pyridin-2-yl)-4,5,6,7-tetrahydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine and 1,3-dichloro-5-isocyanatobenzene. Yield 2 mg (1%). 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6): δ = 9.17 (s, 1H, NH), 8.51 (dd, J = 0.88, 4.67 Hz, 1H, pyridine), 7.77 (dt, J = 1.77, 7.71 Hz, 1H, pyridine), 7.55 (d, J = 2.02 Hz, 2H, dichlorophenyl), 7.24–7.36 (m, 3H, pyridine, thiophene), 7.06–7.14 (m, 1H, dichlorophenyl), 6.91 (d, J = 5.31 Hz, 1H, thiophene), 6.36 (s, 1H, CHN), 4.37 (m, 1H, CH2CH2), 3.33–3.53 (m, 1H, CH2CH2), 2.85–2.97 (m, 2H, CH2CH2). MS (ESI): m/z = 404.3 [M + H]+.

    (5S)-5-(4-Chlorophenyl)-N-[4-(4-fluorophenoxy)phenyl]-7,8-dihydro-1,6-naphthyridine-6(5H)-carboxamide (33, BAY-298)
    Step 1: To a 1.0 M solution of 4-chlorophenylmagnesium bromide (37; 50 mL, 50 mmol) in diethylether was added THF (100 mL). Then, 4-methoxypyridine (39; 4.7 mL, 46 mmol) at −30 °C was added and the mixture was stirred for 30 min at −20 °C, cooled to −50 °C, and benzyl chloroformate (38; 7.8 mL, 55 mmol) was added. The reaction mixture was slowly warmed to −15 °C, ice (100 g) was added, and the mixture was stirred for 1 h. 5 M aq citric acid (100 mL) was added, and the mixture was stirred at rt for 30 min, then extracted with EtOAc (3×). The combined organic layers were dried over Na2SO4, and the volatiles were removed under reduced pressure. The crude material was crystallized from diisopropyl ether to obtain benzyl 2-(4-chlorophenyl)-4-oxo-3,4-dihydropyridine-1(2H)-carboxylate. MS (ES+): m/z = 342 [M + H]+.     Pharmacology

    General Methods

    Compound Logistics
    Small volume, ready-to-use microtiter test plates (Greiner Bio-One, Germany) were prepared in advance for all assays used in screening, hit confirmation, and compound characterization of the human LH, FSH, LGR7, and EP2 receptors as well as the membrane adenylate cyclase (mAC) activity assay. 1536-well screening plates received 50 nL of a 1 mM compound solution, whereas 384-well plates were supplied with 50 nL of test compounds (both in 100% DMSO) using a Hummingbird liquid handler (Digilab, MA). Negative and positive control wells, typically 16 wells in a 384-well plate or 64 wells in a 1536-well plate, received 50 nL of 100% DMSO only. A 10 μM compound concentration was used for screening purposes, followed by single concentration confirmation testing in duplicates at 25 and 5 μM. For the establishment of dose–response curves, compounds were typically tested in duplicates at up to 11 concentrations (e.g., 20 μM, 5.7 μM, 1.6 μM, 0.47 μM, 0.13 μM, 38 nM, 11 nM, 3.1 nM, 0.89 nM, 0.25 nM, and 0.073 nM). Compounds used in species specificity testing were always prepared fresh, starting from a 10 mM solution (in 100% DMSO), and initially diluted 1:10 in 100% DMSO to obtain a 1 mM stock solution. Next, 6 μL of this stock solution was transferred into 300 μL of phosphate-buffered saline (PBS) to generate a 20 μM test solution (in 2% DMSO) used as the starting point for serial dilutions prepared shortly after. A Tecan Freedom Evo instrument (Tecan, Germany) was used to transfer 60 μL thereof into a white, 96-well dilution plate (Thermo Fisher Scientific, Germany, Nunc, PP, #249944) containing 200 μL of PBS/2% DMSO already, to prepare first a 1:3.3 dilution followed by an additional nine alternating 1:3/1:3.3 dilution steps (i.e., 67 μL/60 μL), respectively, being transferred to the next well containing 200 μL of PBS/2% DMSO. A total of 10 serial dilutions were prepared containing a 2-fold concentration of test compound (i.e., 6.0 μM, 2.0 μM, 600 nM, 200 nM, 60 nM, 20 nM, 6.0 nM, 2.0 nM, 0.6 nM, and 0.2 nM). Finally, 5 μL thereof was transferred to a black, small volume, 384-well compound test plate (Greiner Bio-One, #784076) to be tested in quadruplicates (CyBi-Well vario, Analytik Jena, Germany). Negative and positive control wells received 5 μL of PBS/2% DMSO only. Standard deviations for IC50 determinations by this protocol were typically in the range of 15–30%.
    Frozen Cell Assays(20)
    All cell lines used were routinely monitored for the presence of mycoplasma and shown to be free of any contamination. Vials containing frozen cells expressing the appropriate receptor were removed from liquid nitrogen storage and rapidly thawed by placement into a 37 °C water bath. Immediately after thawing, the cells were transferred by gently decanting into a 50 mL Falcon tube containing preheated medium (see individual assay protocols for details). Any liquid remaining in the vials was gently rinsed with preheated medium and also transferred into the Falcon tube. Next, the cells were harvested by centrifugation for 5 min at 18.0g and the supernatant was removed by gentle decanting prior to the slow addition of preheated medium and resuspension of the pellet by mild swirling. A small aliquot was removed for cell counting, and the cells were diluted to the final cell number (see assay protocols). Finally, an aliquot of the fluorescent cAMP tracer (cAMP-d2) stock solution (prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol) was added to achieve a 1:40 dilution step. The cells were used for seeding into assay plates either with or without an additional preincubation step necessary to achieve full functionality (see individual assay protocols for details). With the exception of the hTSH-R antagonism assay and the respective species specificity profiling (see detailed assay protocols below), all steps of the actual primary screening and also of the specific and unspecific off-target assays were performed at rt, and all solutions were added with a Multidrop dispenser (Thermo Labsystems).
    General cAMP HTRF Assay Principle
    Agonist-induced activation of the GPCRs used in cell-based primary screening (hLH-R), species specificity profiling (mLH-R, rLH-R), and specific (hFSH-R, hTSH-R) or unspecific off-target profiling (hRXFP1/LGR7, hEP2 receptor) results in the production of cAMP as is the case in a membrane-based assay using forskolin stimulation to detect unspecific inhibition of mACs. Following lysis of either cells or membranes, any cAMP present competes with the cAMP-d2 tracer for the binding to an europium cryptate-labeled anti-cAMP antibody (Cisbio International; HTRF cAMP assay; see individual assay protocols for details). The maximum signal resulting from time-resolved fluorescence resonance energy transfer (TR-FRET)(21−23) between the detection reagents is obtained in the absence of cellular cAMP. Any decrease in the TR-FRET signal is indicative of agonist-induced receptor or mAC activation, whereas antagonist activity results in a signal increase. Signal quantification is achieved by using an appropriate plate reader (PHERAstar, RUBYstar, ViewLux). Following excitation at 337 nm, any reduction of FRET-induced emissions at 665 nm is indicative of agonist-induced cAMP production. In addition, a second FRET signal at 620 nm, originating from the europium cryptate-labeled anti-cAMP antibody, is used for well internal referencing (well ratio, defined as signal 665 nm/signal 620 nm × 10 000).(21)

    Data Analysis

    Screening results were analyzed and both EC50 and IC50 values were calculated by four-parameter fitting using either a commercial software package (Genedata Screener, Switzerland) or proprietary software developed in-house.

    Human LH-R Antagonism

    (Frozen cell assay, recovery time: ≤60 min, recovery medium: Ham’s F12 supplemented with 5% charcoal-treated serum). Typically, the reaction volume was 6 μL in white, small volume, 1536- or 384-well plates. Four microliters of the cell suspension containing 625 cells/μL (in Ham’s F12 supplemented with 5% charcoal-treated serum) was added to all wells of a ready-to-use test plate. Following preincubation for 20 min, 2 μL of a 3 × EC80 hLH agonist solution (1.20 nM; always prepared fresh from a small aliquot of a 15 μM stock in water stored at −20 °C for further dilutions in Ham’s F12, 5% charcoal-treated serum, 2.25 mM 3-isobutyl-1-methylxanthine (IBMX); luteinizing hormone, human pituitary, iodination grade, Sigma-Aldrich, #869003, biological activity ≥5.0 IU/mg, ≈641.03 ng, purity ≥95%, contaminants: hFSH, hTSH ≤ 0.5%, hCG ≤ 0.05%) was added to the test compound and positive control wells (low controls, [C(0)]). The negative control wells (high controls, [C(i)]) received Ham’s F12, 5% charcoal-treated serum, and 0.75 mM IBMX only. Then, the plate was incubated for another 60 min in the presence of a 1 × EC80 concentration of agonist (0.40 nM). The reaction was stopped by the addition of 2 μL of lysis buffer containing a 1:20 dilution of europium cryptate-labeled anti-cAMP antibody stock prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol (cAMP-Gs femtomolar kit). Another 20 min later, the cell lysate-containing plate was transferred to a TR-FRET compatible reader to quantify the results.

    Rat LH-R Antagonism

    (Frozen cell species specificity assay, recovery time: 60 min, recovery medium: PBS, 0.1% BSA, 0.2% glucose. A 1:40 dilution of the cAMP-d2 tracer (cAMP HiRange Kit, Cisbio, #62AM6PEJ) was added shortly before seeding). The reaction volume was 15 μL in 384-well plates. Five microliters of a rat ovary granulosa GLHR15 cell suspension stably expressing the rat LH-R(44) was seeded to all wells of the compound test plate (2500 cells/well, 500 cells/μL). Following preincubation for 30 min at 37 °C, 5 μL of a 3 × EC90 hLH agonist solution (3.0 nM; Calbiochem, 869003; prepared fresh in PBS, 2.25 mM IBMX) was added to the test compound and positive control wells (low controls, [C(0)]). The negative control wells (high controls, [C(i)]) received PBS, 0.1% BSA, 0.2% glucose, and 0.75 mM IBMX only. Then, the plate was incubated for another 30 min at 37 °C in the presence of a 1 × EC90 concentration of agonist (1.00 nM). The reaction was stopped by the addition of 5 μL of lysis buffer containing a 1:20 dilution of the europium cryptate-labeled anti-cAMP antibody stock prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol (cAMP HiRange Kit, Cisbio, #62AM6PEJ). Another 60 min at rt later, the cell lysate-containing plate was transferred to a TR-FRET compatible reader to quantify the results.

    Mouse LH-R Antagonism

    Mouse Leydig tumor MLTC-1 cells endogenously expressing the mouse LH-R(45) were obtained from routine culture (in RPMI-1640 medium, 10% FCS, 10 mM HEPES, 1 mM sodium pyruvate, 100 U/mL penicillin, 100 μg/mL streptomycin, 4 mM l-glutamine, 2.5 g/L glucose) by Accutase treatment and resuspended at 1000 cells/μL in PBS, 0.1% BSA, and 0.2% glucose to which a 1:40 dilution of the cAMP-d2 tracer (cAMP HiRange Kit, Cisbio, #62AM6PEJ) was added shortly before seeding. Five microliters (i.e., 5000 cells/well) was added to all wells of the compound test plate using a Multidrop Combi instrument (Thermo Fisher Scientific, Germany). Following preincubation for 30 min at 37 °C, 5 μL of a 3 × EC90 hLH agonist solution (1.00 nM; Calbiochem, 869003; prepared fresh in PBS, 2.25 mM IBMX) was added to the test compound and positive control wells (low controls, [C(0)]). The negative control wells (high controls, [C(i)]) received PBS, 0.1% BSA, 0.2% glucose, and 0.75 mM IBMX only. Then, the plate was incubated for another 30 min at 37 °C in the presence of a 1 × EC90 concentration of agonist (0.33 nM). The reaction was stopped by the addition 5 μL of lysis buffer containing a 1:20 dilution of the europium cryptate-labeled anti-cAMP antibody stock prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol. Another 60 min at rt later, the cell lysate-containing plate was transferred to a TR-FRET compatible reader (PHERAstar Plus, BMG Labtech, Germany) to quantify the results.

    Cynomolgus Monkey LH-R Antagonism

    HEK293 human embryonic kidney cells (ATCC CRL-1573) transiently expressing the cynomolgus monkey LH receptor (cLH-R) were prepared using a cationic liposome formulation. Plasmid DNA–lipid complexes were formed by dropwise addition of 2.5 mL of Opti-MEM medium containing 20 μL of an expression vector for cLH-R (pLHR-64 #1, kindly provided by Prof. Jörg Gromoll; 10 ng/μL) to a second vial containing 2.5 mL of Opti-MEM medium and 60 μL of Lipofectamine (Thermo Fisher Scientific, #11686-019), followed by an incubation for 45 min. HEK293 cells grown to 80% confluency in 162 cm2 flasks were washed once with Opti-MEM medium prior to the addition of 15 mL of Opti-MEM medium followed by dropwise addition of 5 mL of Opti-MEM medium containing the DNA–lipid complexes. The cell supernatant was removed following incubation for 5 h at 37 °C, 7% CO2, and replaced by 20 mL of Dulbecco’s modified Eagle’s medium (DMEM) containing 5% charcoal-treated serum for overnight incubation at 37 °C, 7% CO2. The cells were harvested by Accutase treatment (Sigma-Aldrich, #A6964) the next day and used to prepare a cell suspension of 1000 cells/μL in PBS, 0.1% BSA, 0.2% glucose, 2.25 mM IBMX supplemented with a 1:40 dilution of cAMP-d2 solution prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol. Five microliters of this cell suspension (i.e., 5000 cells/well) was added to all wells of the compound test plate using a Multidrop Combi instrument (Thermo Fisher Scientific, Germany). Following preincubation for 30 min at 37 °C, 5 μL of a 3 × EC80 hLH agonist solution (3.00 nM; prepared fresh in PBS, 0.1% BSA, 0.2% glucose, 2.25 mM IBMX) was added to the test compound and positive control wells (low controls, [C(0)]). The negative control wells (high controls, [C(i)]) received PBS, 0.1% BSA, 0.2% glucose, and 2.25 mM IBMX only. Then, the plate was incubated for another 30 min at 37 °C in the presence of a 1 × EC80 concentration of agonist (1.00 nM). The reaction was stopped by the addition of 5 μL of lysis buffer containing a 1:20 dilution of the europium cryptate-labeled anti-cAMP antibody stock prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol. Another 90 min later, the cell lysate-containing plate was transferred to a TR-FRET compatible reader (PHERAstar Plus, BMG Labtech, Germany) to quantify the results.

    Antagonist Dose–Response Studies with Compound 33

    The effect of multiple, single agonist concentrations on the shift in antagonist potency was determined by antagonist dose–response studies using the antagonist dose–response curve obtained in the presence of 1 × EC80 as control. A total of four different agonist concentrations were tested (i.e., 4 ×, 10 ×, 16 ×, and 100 × EC80 of either hLH or the SMOL agonist Org 43553). Typically, the reaction volume was 6 μL in white, small volume, 384-well plates. Four microliters of the cell suspension containing 625 cells/μL (in Ham’s F12 supplemented with 5% charcoal-treated serum) was added to all wells of a ready-to-use test plate containing a 1:3.3 serial dilution of the antagonist (i.e., 17 μM, 5.5 μM, 1.8 μM, 610 nM, 200 nM, 68 nM, 23 nM, 7.5 nM, 2.5 nM, and 0.84 nM). Following preincubation for 20 min, 2 μL containing the given concentration of agonist was added to the test compound and positive control wells (low controls, [C(0)]). The negative control wells (high controls, [C(i)]) received Ham’s F12, 5% charcoal-treated serum, and 0.75 mM IBMX only. Then, the plate was incubated for another 60 min in the presence of agonist. The reaction was stopped by the addition of 2 μL of lysis buffer containing a 1:20 dilution of europium cryptate-labeled anti-cAMP antibody stock prepared according to the manufacturer’s protocol (cAMP-Gs femtomolar kit). Another 20 min later, the cell lysate-containing plate was transferred to a TR-FRET compatible reader to quantify the results.

    X-ray Structure Analysis of Compound 5

    Data for 5 were collected at 100 K on a Bruker SMART 6000 system equipped with a CCD area detector and Cu X-ray radiation (Cu Kα, λ = 1.54178 Å). X-ray data collection and processing of data were performed using the SHELXTL package (SHELXTL Version 6.14, Bruker AXS Inc.: Madison, Wisconsin, 2012). SHELXM was used for structure solution and SHELXL was used for full-matrix least-squares refinement on F2.(47) All nonhydrogen atoms were refined anisotropically. All hydrogen atoms were placed in geometrically ideal positions using the riding model. The hydrogen atom attached at N11 was identified as a difference peak in the Fourier analysis and refined without restraints. The isotropic temperature factors of all hydrogen atoms were 1.2 and 1.5 times the size of the temperature factors of the corresponding heavy atoms. The program XP from SHELXTL Version 6.14 and Discovery Studio 2018 was used for all molecular representations (SHELXTL Version 6.14, Bruker AXS Inc.: Madison, Wisconsin, 2012; Dassault Systèmes BIOVIA, Discovery Studio, Version 18.1.100.18065, Dassault Systèmes: San Diego, 2017).
    Colorless plates of 5 were obtained by slow evaporation of a 3:4 mixture of hexane/n-butanol solution of the compound at rt. A single crystal with dimensions 0.1 × 0.05 × 0.05 mm3 was mounted on a CryoLoop using protective oil. One independent molecule of 5 crystallizes in the orthorhombic space group P212121 (Z = 4) with cell constants a = 8.1494(13) Å, b = 10.0083(18) Å, c = 23.823(4) Å. The molecular formula is C22H22N2O3S with a molecular weight of 394.48 g/mol. A total of 13 444 reflections, of which 2250 are unique (Rint = 0.0637), were collected. The final R values were R1 = 0.0491, I > 2σ(I), and wR2 = 0.1327 for all data. The goodness-of-fit of the data was 1.064. The absolute structure has been determined with a Flack parameter of 0.01(4).(48) The crystallographic data for 5 have been deposited with the Cambridge Crystallographic Data Centre (CCDC) with deposition code CCDC 1943802.

    Physicochemical Assays

    Stability of Compounds in Solution (pH 10, 7, and 1, at 37 °C)

    Solution stability was determined by HPLC-UV.(49) Five microliters of a 10 mM solution of compound in DMSO was dissolved in 1 mL of acetonitrile. One hundred microliters of this solution was transferred to 1 mL of the respective buffer and mixed thoroughly. Injections were made immediately after mixing for time zero injection and then again after 1, 2, and 24 h. Compounds were incubated at 37 °C. Degradation rate (recovery in %) was calculated by relating the peak areas after 1, 2, and 24 h to the time zero injection.

    Aqueous Solubility of Compounds in DMSO Solutions

    Aqueous solubility at pH 6.5 was determined by an orientating HTS method.(50) Test compounds were applied as 1 mM DMSO solutions. After addition of buffer pH 6.5, solutions were shaken for 24 h at rt. Undissolved material was removed by filtration. The compound dissolved in the filtrate was quantified by HPLC-MS/MS.

    Log D Measurement

    Log D values at pH 7.5 were recorded using an indirect method for determining hydrophobicity constants by reversed-phase HPLC.(51) A homologous series of n-alkan-2-ones (C3–C16, 0.02 M in acetonitrile) was used for calibration. Test compounds were applied as 0.67 mM DMSO stock solutions in acetonitrile/water (1:1). The lipophilicity of compounds was then assessed by comparison to the calibration curve.

    Pharmacokinetic Assays

    Caco-2 Permeability Assay

    Caco-2 cells (purchased from DSMZ Braunschweig, Germany) were seeded at a density of 4.5 × 104 cells/well on 24-well insert plates, 0.4 μm pore size, and grown for 15 days in DMEM supplemented with 10% FCS, 1% GlutaMAX (100 ×, Gibco), 100 U/mL penicillin, 100 μg/mL streptomycin (Gibco), and 1% nonessential amino acids (100×). The cells were maintained at 37 °C in a humidified 5% CO2 atmosphere. The medium was changed every 2–3 days. Before the permeation assay was run, the culture medium was replaced by FCS-free HEPES carbonate transport buffer (pH 7.2). For the assessment of monolayer integrity, the transepithelial electrical resistance was measured. Test compounds were predissolved in DMSO and added either to the apical or basolateral compartment at a final concentration of 2 μM. Before and after incubation for 2 h at 37 °C, samples were taken from both compartments and analyzed by LC-MS/MS after precipitation with MeOH. Permeability (Papp) was calculated in the apical to basolateral (A → B) and basolateral to apical (B → A) directions. The apparent permeability was calculated using the following equation: Papp = (Vr/P0)(1/S)(P2/t), where Vr is the volume of medium in the receiver chamber, P0 is the measured peak area of the test drug in the donor chamber at t = 0, S is the surface area of the monolayer, P2 is the measured peak area of the test drug in the acceptor chamber after incubation for 2 h, and t is the incubation time. The efflux ratio basolateral (B) to apical (A) was calculated as Papp B–A/Papp A–B. In addition, the compound recovery was calculated. As an assay control, reference compounds were analyzed in parallel.

    In Vitro Metabolic Stability in Human Liver Microsomes

    The in vitro metabolic stability of test compounds was determined by incubation at 1 μM with a suspension of liver microsomes (purchased from Xenotech) in 100 mM phosphate buffer, pH 7.4 (NaH2PO4·H2O + Na2HPO4·2H2O) at a protein concentration of 0.5 mg/mL at 37 °C. The microsomes were activated by adding a cofactor mix containing 8 mM glucose-6-phosphate (G6P), 4 mM MgCl2, 0.5 mM NADP, and 1 IU/mL G6P dehydrogenase in phosphate buffer (pH 7.4). The metabolic assay was started shortly afterward by adding the test compound to the incubation at a final volume of 1 mL. Organic solvent in the incubations was limited to ≤0.01% DMSO and ≤1% acetonitrile. During incubation, the microsomal suspensions were continuously shaken at 580 rpm and aliquots were taken at 2, 8, 16, 30, 45, and 60 min, to which an equal volume of cold MeOH was immediately added. Samples were frozen at −20 °C overnight, subsequently centrifuged for 15 min at 3000 rpm, and the supernatant was analyzed with an Agilent 1200 HPLC system with LC-MS/MS detection. The half-life of a test compound was determined from the concentration–time plot. From the half-life, the intrinsic clearance was calculated. Together with the additional parameters liver blood flow, specific liver weight, and microsomal protein content, the hepatic in vivo blood clearance (CL) and the maximal oral bioavailability (Fmax) were calculated using the “well-stirred” liver model. The following parameter values were used: liver blood flow, 1.32 L/(h kg); specific liver weight, 21 g/kg body weight; microsomal protein content, 40 mg/g. For classification of the results, the following criteria were used: Fmax > 70% (=CL < 0.4 L/(h kg)) was classified as high metabolic stability (high), Fmax = 30–70% (CL 0.4–0.9 L/(h kg)) as moderate stability, and Fmax < 30% (CL > 0.9 L/(h kg)) as low metabolic stability

    In Vitro Metabolic Stability in Rat Liver Microsomes

    The in vitro metabolic stability of test compounds was determined by incubation at 1 μM with a suspension of liver microsomes (purchased from Xenotech) in 100 mM phosphate buffer, pH 7.4 (NaH2PO4·H2O + Na2HPO4·2H2O), and at a protein concentration of 0.5 mg/mL at 37 °C. The microsomes were activated by adding a cofactor mix containing 8 mM G6P, 4 mM MgCl2, 0.5 mM NADP, and 1 IU/mL G6P dehydrogenase in phosphate buffer (pH 7.4). The metabolic assay was started shortly afterward by adding the test compound to the incubation at a final volume of 1 mL. Organic solvent in the incubations was limited to ≤ 0.01% DMSO and ≤ 1% acetonitrile. During incubation, the microsomal suspensions were continuously shaken at 580 rpm and aliquots were taken at 2, 8, 16, 30, 45, and 60 min, to which an equal volume of cold MeOH was immediately added. Samples were frozen at −20 °C overnight, subsequently centrifuged for 15 min at 3000 rpm, and the supernatant was analyzed with an Agilent 1200 HPLC system with LC-MS/MS detection. The half-life of a test compound was determined from the concentration–time plot. From the half-life, the intrinsic clearance was calculated. Together with the additional parameters liver blood flow, specific liver weight, and microsomal protein content, the hepatic in vivo blood clearance (CL) and the maximal oral bioavailability (Fmax) were calculated using the well-stirred liver model. The following parameter values were used: liver blood flow, 4.2 L/(h kg); specific liver weight, 32 g/kg rat body weight; microsomal protein content, 40 mg/g.

    In Vitro Metabolic Stability in Rat Hepatocytes

    Hepatocytes from Han Wistar rats (purchased from Harlan, NL) were isolated via a two-step perfusion method. After perfusion, the liver was carefully removed from the rat, the liver capsule was opened, and the hepatocytes were gently shaken out into a Petri dish with ice-cold Williams’ medium E (WME). The resulting cell suspension was filtered through sterile gauze into 50 mL Falcon tubes and centrifuged at 50g for 3 min at rt. The cell pellet was resuspended in 30 mL of WME and centrifuged through a Percoll gradient two times at 100g. The hepatocytes were washed again with WME and resuspended in a medium containing 5% FCS. Cell viability was determined by trypan blue exclusion. For the metabolic stability assay, liver cells were distributed in WME containing 5% FCS to glass vials at a density of 1.0 × 106 vital cells/mL. The test compound was added at a final concentration of 1 μM. During incubation, the hepatocyte suspensions were continuously shaken at 580 rpm and aliquots were taken at 2, 8, 16, 30, 45, and 90 min, to which an equal volume of cold MeOH was immediately added. Samples were frozen at −20 °C overnight, subsequently centrifuged for 15 min at 3000 rpm, and the supernatant was analyzed with an Agilent 1200 HPLC system with LC-MS/MS detection. The half-life of a test compound was determined from the concentration–time plot. From the half-life, the intrinsic clearance was calculated using the well-stirred liver model together with the additional parameters liver blood flow and amount of liver cells in vivo and in vitro. The hepatic in vivo blood clearance (CL) and the maximal oral bioavailability (Fmax) were calculated. The following parameter were used: liver blood flow, 4.2 L/(h kg) rat; specific liver weight, 32 g/kg rat body weight; liver cells in vivo, 1.1 × 108 cells/g liver; liver cells in vitro, 1.0 × 106/mL.

    Inhibition of CYP450 Metabolism

    The inhibitory potency of test compounds toward cytochrome P450-dependent metabolic pathways was determined in human liver microsomes (purchased from Xenotech) by applying individual CYP isoform selective standard probes (CYP1A2, phenacetin; CYP2C8, amodiaquine; CYP2C9, diclofenac; CYP2D6, dextromethorphan; CYP3A4, midazolam). Reference inhibitors were included as positive controls. Incubation conditions (protein and substrate concentration, incubation time) were optimized with regard to linearity of metabolite formation. Assays were processed in 96-well plates at 37 °C using a Genesis Workstation (Tecan, Crailsheim, Germany). After protein precipitation, metabolite formation was quantified by LC-MS/MS analysis, which was followed by inhibition evaluation and IC50 calculation.

    Binding to Plasma Proteins

    The binding of test compounds to plasma proteins was measured using the method reported by Schuhmacher et al.(52)

    In Vivo Pharmacokinetics in Rats

    All animal studies were conducted in accordance with the German Animal Welfare Act and the ethical guidelines of Bayer AG and were approved by the local ethics committee. Female and male Wistar rats were obtained from Charles River (Germany) and had access to food and water ad libitum. All animals were housed according to institutional guidelines under a 12/12 h light/dark cycle and maintained under standard conditions (20–22 °C, 50–70% humidity). The rats were housed in Makrolon cages type IV, five animals per cage, fed a pelleted diet (Ssniff, Germany), and used for in vivo studies with a weight of 200–230 g (average, 220 g).
    For in vivo pharmacokinetic experiments, test compounds were administered to female Wistar rats intravenously at a dose of 0.5 mg/kg and po at a dose of 2.0 mg/kg formulated as solutions using solubilizers such as PEG400 and EtOH in well-tolerated amounts. Blood samples were collected, for example, at 2 min (iv only), 8 min, 15 min, 30 min, 45 min, 1 h, 2 h, 4 h, 6 h, 8 h, 12 h, 14 h, 18 h, 24 h, 48 h, and 72 h after dosing from the vena jugularis into lithium heparin tubes (Monovette, Sarstedt) and centrifuged for 15 min at 3000 rpm. An aliquot of 100 μL from the supernatant (plasma) was taken and precipitated by the addition of 400 μL of cold acetonitrile. Samples were frozen at −20 °C overnight and subsequently thawed and centrifuged at 3000 rpm and 4 °C for 20 min. Aliquots of the supernatant were analyzed with an Agilent HPLC system with LC-MS/MS detection. Pharmacokinetic parameters were calculated by noncompartmental analysis using pharmacokinetics calculation software (e.g., Phoenix WinNonlin, Certara USA, Inc.).

    Automated hERG K+ Current Voltage-Clamp Safety Assay

    The hERG K+ current assay is based on a recombinant HEK293 cell line with stable expression of the KCNH2 (HERG) gene.(53) The cells were cultured using a humidified incubator (37 °C, 5% CO2) and a standard culture medium (MEM with Earle’s salts and l-glutamine, 10% noninactivated FCS, 0.1 mM nonessential amino acids, 1 mM sodium pyruvate, penicillin/streptomycin (50 μg/mL each), 0.4 mg/mL Geneticin). About 0.5–8 h following cell dissociation, the cells were investigated by means of the “whole-cell voltage-clamp” technique(54) in an automated eight-channel system (Patchliner; Nanion Technologies, Munich, Germany) with PatchControlHT software (Nanion) to control the Patchliner system and to handle data acquisition and analysis. Voltage-clamp control was provided by two EPC 10 Quadro amplifiers under control of the PatchMaster Pro software (both: HEKA Elektronik, Lambrecht, Germany) and with NPC-16 medium resistance (∼2 MΩ) chips (Nanion) serving as planar substrate at rt (22–24 °C). NPC-16 chips were filled with intra- and extracellular solution (intracellular solution: 10 mM NaCl, 50 mM KCl, 60 mM KF, 20 mM EGTA, 10 mM HEPES, pH 7.2 (KOH); extracellular solution: 140 mM NaCl, 4 mM KCl, 2 mM CaCl2, 1 mM MgCl2, 5 mM glucose, 10 mM HEPES, pH 7.4 (NaOH)) and with cell suspension. After formation of a GΩ seal and entering whole-cell mode (including several automated quality control steps), the cell membrane was clamped to the holding potential (−80 mV). Following an activating clamp step (+20 mV, 1000 ms), exclusively hERG-mediated inward tail currents were elicited by hyperpolarizing voltage steps from +20 to −120 mV (duration 500 ms); this clamp protocol was repeated every 12 s.(55) After an initial stabilization phase (5–6 min), test compounds were added either as a single concentration (10 μM) or in ascending concentrations (0.1, 1, and 10 μM; 5–6 min per concentration), followed by several washout steps. Effects of test compounds were quantified by analyzing the amplitude of the hERG-mediated inward tail currents (in % of predrug control) as a function of test compound concentration (Igor Pro Software). Mean concentration–response data were fitted with a standard sigmoidal four-parameter logistic equation of the form: Y = Bottom + (Top – Bottom)/(1 + 10∧((log IC50 – X) × HillSlope)), where Y is the current inhibition (in % of predrug control), X is the logarithm of drug concentration, and IC50 is the drug concentration producing half-maximal current inhibition, and using the following constraints: Top = 100%, Bottom = 0%. No curve fitting was performed in cases with an obvious lack of a concentration-dependent current inhibition and/or a too small effect size (ca. ≤20%).

    Gene Expression

    Preparation of Total RNA

    Frozen whole left and right ovaries of each animal were homogenized together in 0.8 mL of RLT buffer (QIAGEN) using a Precellys24 homogenizer (2.8 mm ceramic beads, 2 × 30 s, 6000 rpm, 20 s break). Isolation of total RNA from 0.4 mL of homogenate was performed using a QIAsymphony automat (QIAGEN). Isolated RNA was digested by DNase I (amplification grade, Invitrogen).

    Real-Time Quantitative PCR

    Five microliters (2–5 μg) of DNase I digested RNA was transcribed into cDNA using SuperScript III reverse transcriptase and 50 ng of random hexamer primers (SuperScript III First-Strand Synthesis System, Invitrogen). For relative quantification of mRNA levels, TaqMan PCR was carried out with an SDS 7900HT Sequence Detection System (Applied Biosystems). PCRs were performed with 0.8 μL (50–200 ng) of cDNA using Platinum Quantitative PCR SuperMix-UDG (Invitrogen) under standard reaction conditions. The following available TaqMan Gene Expression Assays (Applied Biosystems) were used: COX-2 (Ptgs2) (Assay ID: Rn00568225_m1), Egr-1 (Assay ID: Rn00561138_m1), RUNX-1 (Assay ID: Rn00569082_m1), Cyp19A1 (aromatase) (Assay ID: Rn01422547_m1), PPIA (cyclophilin A) (Assay ID: Rn00690933_m1). Relative mRNA levels were calculated by the comparative ΔCT method. Therefore, cyclophilin A (PPIA) was used as endogenous control and the vehicle group was used as calibrator.

    Measurement of Hormone Concentrations

    Plasma estradiol, progesterone, and testosterone were measured by specific radioimmunoassay methods according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Diagnostic Systems Laboratories, Inc.) as described previously.(56,57) Reagents were provided by the World Health Organization under the Programme for the Provision of Matched Assay Reagents for the Radioimmunoassay of Hormones in Reproductive Physiology.

    Animal Treatments

    For short-term experiments, female and male rats were treated with the GnRH antagonist cetrorelix acetate in isotonic Ringer’s solution supplemented with 0.1% BSA (Ringer/0.1% BSA), twice: at 17 and 1 h before the experiment (100 mg/rat in 0.1 mL; IM and sc, respectively). Rats were injected with 0.5 μg (female rats) and 0.3 or 0.1 μg (male rats) of hLH in Ringer/0.1% BSA, with a volume of 0.5 mL iv, with or without 33 (BAY-298, 1-6 mg/animal, average animal weight were 220 g). Autopsy for ovarian analysis was performed after 4 h, and blood samples taken after 2 h for testosterone measurements.(37)
    Compound 33 (BAY-298) or 36 (BAY-899) was dissolved for q.d. po application of a final volume of 0.5 mL in Myrj53 (Polyoxyl 50 stearate, CAS-RN: [9004-99-3]) in 0.9% w/v NaCl solution (85 mg/100 mL) to achieve the different daily doses. Four to five animals were used per group in long-term experiments for 8 days in normally cycling female rats. Female rats were cycle-staged by vaginal smears(38) and treatment started in estrus.

    Statistical Analysis

    Results are expressed as mean + standard deviation, and n represents the number of animals. Statistical significance of differences was tested by one-way analysis of variance (multiple comparisons vs control group) with post hoc Dunnett’s method (p < 0.05).

These include the way hypothalamus regulates pituitary function, and the way pituitary, in turn, affects gonadal function. Finally, age plays an important role in axis regulation, in both genders.

      3
      The hypothalamus-pituitary-gonad axis: Tales of mice and men
      Kaprara, Athina; Huhtaniemi, Ilpo T.
      Metabolism, Clinical and Experimental (2018), 86 (), 3-17CODEN: METAAJ; ISSN:0026-0495. (Elsevier)
      A review. Reprodn. is controlled by the hypothalamic-pituitary-gonadal (HPG) axis. Gonadotropin-releasing hormone (GnRH) neurons play a central role in this axis through prodn. of GnRH, which binds to a membrane receptor on pituitary gonadotrophs and stimulates the biosynthesis and secretion of FSH (FSH) and LH (LH). Multiple factors affect GnRH neuron migration, GnRH gene expression, GnRH pulse generator, GnRH secretion, GnRH receptor expression, and gonadotropin synthesis and release. Among them anosmin is involved in the guidance of the GnRH neuron migration, and a loss-of-function mutation in its gene leads to a failure of their migration from the olfactory placode to the hypothalamus, with consequent anosmic hypogonadotropic hypogonadism (Kallmann syndrome). There are also cases of hypogonadotropic hypogonadim with normal sense of smell, due to mutations of other genes. Another protein, kisspeptin plays a crucial role in the regulation of GnRH pulse generator and the pubertal development. GnRH is the main hypothalamic regulator of the release of gonadotropins. Finally, FSH and LH are the essential hormonal regulators of testicular functions, acting through their receptors in Sertoli and Leydig cells, resp. The main features of the male HPG axis will be described in this review.

      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
      4
      Therapeutic advances in hormone-dependent cancers: focus on prostate, breast and ovarian cancers
      Ulm, Michael; Ramesh, Arvind V.; McNamara, Keely M.; Ponnusamy, Suriyan; Sasano, Hironobu; Narayanan, Ramesh
      Endocrine Connections (2019), 8 (2), R10-R26CODEN: ECNOCX; ISSN:2049-3614. (Bioscientifica Ltd.)
      A review. Hormonal cancers affect over 400,000 men and women and contribute collectively to over 100,000 deaths in the United States alone. Thanks to advances in the understanding of these cancers at the mol. level and to the discovery of several disease-modifying therapeutics, the last decade has seen a plateauing or even a decreasing trend in the no. of deaths from these cancers. These advanced therapeutics not only effectively slow the growth of hormonal cancers, but also provide an insight on how these cancers become refractory and evolve as an altogether distinct subset. This review summarizes the current therapeutic trends in hormonal cancers, with focus on prostate, breast and ovarian cancers. The review discusses the clin. drugs being used now, promising mols. that are going through various stages of development and makes some predictions on how the therapeutic landscape will shift in the next decade.

      Hormones and pathogenesis of uterine fibroids
      Reis Fernando M; Bloise Enrrico; Ortiga-Carvalho Tania M
      Best practice & research. Clinical obstetrics & gynaecology (2016), 34 (), 13-24 ISSN:.
      The role of ovarian steroid hormones in the pathogenesis of uterine fibroids is supported by epidemiological, clinical, and experimental evidence. Estradiol and progesterone induce mature leiomyoma cells to release mitogenic stimuli to adjacent immature cells, thereby providing uterine leiomyoma with undifferentiated cells that are likely to support tumor growth. Progesterone action is required for the complete development and proliferation of leiomyoma cells, while estradiol predominantly increases tissue sensitivity to progesterone by increasing the availability of progesterone receptors (PRs). The selective estrogen receptor modulator (SERM) raloxifene and the selective PR modulators (SPRMs) mifepristone, asoprisnil, and ulipristal acetate have been shown in clinical trials to inhibit fibroid growth. The role of sex steroids is critical for leiomyoma development and maintenance, but a number of autocrine and paracrine messengers are involved in this process; hence, numerous pathways remain to be explored in therapeutic innovations for treating this common disease.

      The G-protein-coupled receptors in the human genome form five main families. Phylogenetic analysis, paralogon groups, and fingerprints
      Fredriksson, Robert; Lagerstrom, Malin C.; Lundin, Lars-Gustav; Schioth, Helgi B.
      Molecular Pharmacology (2003), 63 (6), 1256-1272CODEN: MOPMA3; ISSN:0026-895X. (American Society for Pharmacology and Experimental Therapeutics)
      The superfamily of G-protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs) is very diverse in structure and function and its members are among the most pursued targets for drug development. We identified more than 800 human GPCR sequences and simultaneously analyzed 342 unique functional nonolfactory human GPCR sequences with phylogenetic analyses. Our results show, with high bootstrap support, five main families, named glutamate, rhodopsin, adhesion, frizzled/taste2, and secretin, forming the GRAFS classification system. The rhodopsin family is the largest and forms four main groups with 13 subbranches. Positions of the GPCRs in chromosomal paralogons regions indicate the importance of tetraploidizations or local gene duplication events for their creation. We also searched for "fingerprint" motifs using Hidden Markov Models delineating the putative inter-relationship of the GRAFS families. We show several common structural features indicating that the human GPCRs in the GRAFS families share a common ancestor. This study represents the first overall map of the GPCRs in a single mammalian genome. Our novel approach of analyzing such large and diverse sequence sets may be useful for studies on GPCRs in other genomes and divergent protein families.

      Involvement of Gs and Gi proteins in dual coupling of the luteinizing hormone receptor to adenylyl cyclase and phospholipase C
      Herrlich, Andreas; Kuehn, Bernhard; Grosse, Robert; Schmid, Andrea; Schultz, Guenter; Gudermann, Thomas
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (1996), 271 (28), 16764-16772CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      Binding of lutropin/choriogonadotropin to its cognate receptor results in the activation of adenylyl cyclase and phospholipase C. The mechanism underlying the generation of this bifurcating signal is presently not known. To analyze the coupling mechanism of the LH receptor, activated G proteins were labeled with [α-32P]GTP azidoanilide and identified by selective immunopptn. In membranes of bovine corpora lutea and of L cells stably expressing the murine LH receptor (LHR cells), human chorionic gonadotropin (hCG) led to incorporation of the label into αs and αi2. Stimulation of LHR cells or of L cells expressing the M5 muscarinic receptor (LM5 cells) with the resp. agonist resulted in activation of phospholipase C in both cell lines. However, αq and α11 were only labeled upon stimulation of the M5 muscarinic receptor. Agonist-induced Ca2+ mobilization and inositol phosphate accumulation were partially sensitive to pertussis toxin, and the expression of the βγ-stimulable phospholipase C isoforms β2 and β3 could be demonstrated in LHR cells. Overexpression of phospholipase C-β2 led to increased hCG-stimulated inositol phosphate accumulation, and expression of a β-ARK1 C-terminal polypeptide effectively suppressed hCG-mediated phosphatidylinositol hydrolysis. Thus, the LH receptor couples to both Gs and Gi, and βγ-subunits released from either G protein contribute to the stimulation of phospholipase C-β isoforms.

      The Luteinizing Hormone Receptor Activates Phospholipase C via Preferential Coupling to Gi2
      Kuehn, Bernhard; Gudermann, Thomas
      Biochemistry (1999), 38 (38), 12490-12498CODEN: BICHAW; ISSN:0006-2960. (American Chemical Society)
      Binding of lutropin/choriogonadotropin (LH/CG) to its cognate receptor results in the activation of adenylyl cyclase and phospholipase C. This divergent signaling of the LH receptor is based on the independent activation of distinct G protein subfamilies, i.e., Gs, Gi, and potentially also Gq. To examine the selectivity of LH receptor coupling to phospholipase C β-activating G proteins, we used an in vivo reconstitution system based on the coexpression of the LH receptor and different G proteins in baculovirus-infected insect cells. In this paper, we describe a refined expression strategy for the LH receptor in insect cells. The receptor protein was inserted into the cell membrane at an expression level of 0.8 pmol/mg of membrane protein. Sf9 cells expressing the LH receptor responded to hCG challenge with a concn.-dependent accumulation of intracellular cAMP (EC50 = 630 nM) but not of inositol phosphates, whereas stimulation of the histamine H1 receptor in Sf9 cells led to increased phospholipase C (PLC) activity. Immunoblotting expts. using G protein-specific antisera revealed the absence of quant. amts. of αi in Sf9 cells, whereas αs and αq/11 were detected. We therefore attempted to restore the hCG-dependent PLC activation by infection of Sf9 cells with viruses encoding the LH receptor and different G protein α subunits. The hCG stimulation of cells coexpressing the LH receptor and exogenous αi2 resulted in stimulation of PLC activity. In cells coinfected with an αi3-baculovirus, hCG challenge led to a minor activation of PLC, whereas no hCG-dependent PLC stimulation was obsd. in cells coexpressing αi1. Most notably, coinfection with baculoviruses encoding αq or α11 did not reproduce the PLC activation by the LH receptor. Thus, the murine LH receptor activates adenylyl cyclase via Gs and PLC via selective coupling to Gi2.

      Persistent cAMP signaling by internalized LH receptors in ovarian follicles
      Lyga, Sandra; Volpe, Silvia; Werthmann, Ruth C.; Goetz, Konrad; Sungkaworn, Titiwat; Lohse, Martin J.; Calebiro, Davide
      Endocrinology (2016), 157 (4), 1613-1621CODEN: ENDOAO; ISSN:1945-7170. (Endocrine Society)
      A crucial event in female reprodn. occurs at midcycle, when a LH peak induces the final maturation of ovarian follicles. LH signals via a G protein-coupled receptor selectively expressed in the outermost follicular cell layers. However, how LH signals are relayed inside these cells and finally to the oocyte is incompletely understood. Here, we monitored LH signaling in intact ovarian follicles of transgenic mice expressing a fluorescent cAMP sensor. We found that LH stimulation induces 2 phases of cAMP signaling in all cell layers surrounding the oocyte. Interfering with LH receptor internalization abolished the second, persistent cAMP phase and partially inhibited oocyte meiosis resumption. These data suggest that persistent cAMP signals from internalized LH receptors contribute to transmitting LH effects inside follicle cells and ultimately to the oocyte. Thus, this study indicates that the recently proposed paradigm of cAMP signaling by internalized G protein-coupled receptors is implicated in receptor function and is physiol. relevant.

      Spare Luteinizing Hormone Receptors: Facts and Fiction
      Casarini, Livio; Santi, Daniele; Simoni, Manuela; Poti, Francesco
      Trends in Endocrinology and Metabolism (2018), 29 (4), 208-217CODEN: TENME4; ISSN:1043-2760. (Elsevier Ltd.)
      A review. It is common opinion that maximal activation of LH (LH)-dependent steroidogenic signal occurs at <1% of human LH/choriogonadotropin (hCG) receptor (LHCGR) occupancy. This effect would be a consequence of an excess of receptors expressed on the surface of theca cells, resulting in a pool of LHCGRs remaining unbound (spare). This concept was borrowed from historical pharmacol. studies, when discrepancies between ligand-receptor binding and dose-response curves of cAMP were evaluated by treating mouse or rat Leydig cells with hCG in vitro. Recent findings demonstrated the specificity of LH- and hCG-dependent effects, receptor heterodimerization, and differing behaviors of rodent vs. human gonadotropin-responsive cells, which may help to revise the 'spare' LHCGRs concept applied to human ovarian physiol. and assisted reprodn.

      [3H]Org 43553, the first low-molecular-weight agonistic and allosteric radioligand for the human luteinizing hormone receptor
      Heitman, Laura H.; Oosterom, Julia; Bonger, Kimberly M.; Timmers, Cornelis M.; Wiegerinck, Peter H. G.; IJzerman, Adriaan P.
      Molecular Pharmacology (2008), 73 (2), 518-524CODEN: MOPMA3; ISSN:0026-895X. (American Society for Pharmacology and Experimental Therapeutics)
      The LH receptor plays a pivotal role in reprodn. The high-mol.-wt. (HMW) human chorionic gonadotropin (hCG) and LH are the endogenous ligands of this receptor and bind to its large N terminus. The present study characterizes the binding of a new low-mol.-wt. (LMW) radioligand, [3H]5-amino-2-methylsulfanyl-4-[3-(2-morpholin-4-yl-acetylamino)- phenyl]-thieno[2,3-d]pyrimidine-6-carboxylic acid tert-butylamide (Org 43553), at the LH receptor. Equil. satn. and displacement assays were developed and optimized. Specific binding of [3H]Org 43553 to CHO-K1 cell membranes expressing the human LH receptor and a cAMP response element-luciferase reporter gene was saturable with a KD value of 2.4±0.4 nM and a Bmax value of 1.6±0.2 pmol/mg protein. Affinities of five LMW analogs of Org 43553 were detd. All displaced the radioligand competitively, with Ki values ranging from 3.3 to 100 nM. Finally, the potency of these compds. in a cAMP-induced luciferase assay was also detd. There was a high correlation between affinity and potency (r = 0.99; P < 0.0001) of these compds. In the search for LMW ligands, which bind allosterically to the seven-transmembrane domain of the LH receptor, a HMW radioligand (e.g., 125I-hCG) is not suitable as it is not displaced by a LMW compd. Therefore, [3H]Org 43553, a new radioligand with good binding properties, allows screening for new LMW ligands that mimic the action of the endogenous hormone at the LH receptor.

      HTRF: pioneering technology for high-throughput screening

      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
